    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3304537663" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3304537663" />
    <a title="Jon Ureña" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3073125-jon-ure-a"><img alt="Jon Ureña" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584386636p2/3073125.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3304537663?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jon Ureña" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jon Ureña" href="/user/show/3073125-jon-ure-a">Jon Ureña</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3073125-jon-ure-a?shelf=mental-illness">mental-illness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3073125-jon-ure-a?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3304537663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6534071109061935853">Three stars for the story and how it was handled, an additional star for the prose.<br/><br/>A few years ago I read this author's "Coin Locker Babies", which I had heard was the best novel he had to offer. I enjoyed it a lot, although I remember very little about it beyond it being generally disturbing. Immediately before this novel I'm reviewing I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3328514775?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>"Audition"</a>, also by this author. The ending sequence of both "Audience" and "Piercing" are too similar for comfort, which exacerbates this author's shortc</span>
  <span id="freeText6534071109061935853" style="display:none">Three stars for the story and how it was handled, an additional star for the prose.<br /><br />A few years ago I read this author's "Coin Locker Babies", which I had heard was the best novel he had to offer. I enjoyed it a lot, although I remember very little about it beyond it being generally disturbing. Immediately before this novel I'm reviewing I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3328514775?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">"Audition"</a>, also by this author. The ending sequence of both "Audience" and "Piercing" are too similar for comfort, which exacerbates this author's shortcomings.<br /><br />We are introduced to the main protagonist as he stands over his sleeping baby daughter, while he struggles to contain himself from stabbing the baby's face with an ice pick. The author proceeds to tell us that the protagonist has issues. As a child he suffered continuous physical abuse from his mother to the point that he developed a dissociative disorder, which allowed him to feel like someone else was enduring the pain while he loitered somewhere else. Shortly after he became an adult, his mother begged for forgiveness, but he slapped her. That was the last time he interacted with her. He also spent a few years, I'm not clear when, in a home for extremely fucked up children/teenagers. He describes how some of the kids constantly banged their heads against walls, cut themselves, attacked others, and some of the sexually abused females attempted to snuggle with any male adult present and shove his hand in her underwear. In his first years as a young adult, the protagonist lived with a stripper who was like twenty years older. She used to bring men home and fuck them in front of him, and afterwards she berated the protagonist for failing to get mad and stop her. The relationship ended when the protagonist, after another bout of mutual physical abuse that she started, ended up stabbing the stripper with an ice pick. She survived, but whatever sort of relationship they had ended. From then on the protagonist survived by solidifying an acceptable persona for himself, one that could keep a job and be passable for common people. He ended up dating some normal enough girl, and even had a child with her. However, the nightmares from his previous life keep resurfacing. He has an extreme urge to murder someone, preferably some female surrogate of his mother, and he believes that he won't be able to keep it together until he gets these urges out of his system. He resolves to get some time off work, travel somewhere and murder some unknown woman.<br /><br />This whole setup reminds me of Patricia Highsmith's books (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1739324969?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">"This Sweet Sickness"</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2034827660?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">"The Cry of the Owl"</a>), that feature plenty of characters with mental "configurations", so to speak, that would never allow them to survive by themselves on the real world, so they base all their interactions on wearing a mask, and if the people around them really knew what those characters were doing, the people in their lives would lose their shit. Highsmith knew plenty about that, given that she endured being different for her entire life without having an explanation as to why; she was diagnosed post-mortem by a psychologist acquaintance as having high-functioning autism (mentioned in the biography <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1739607173?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">"Beautiful Shadow: A Life of Patricia Highsmith"</a>). I really should read more of her stuff.<br /><br />Now that we've moved past the setup, I'll include some untagged mild spoilers. The protagonist checks into some hotel, and we follow his thought process as he plans how to murder some prostitute. He intends to call an S&amp;M girl, tie her up as part of the job, cut her Achilles tendons to hear the snapping sound, and finally stab into her chest with an ice pick enough times so she bleeds to death in front of him. Nobody will miss a prostitute. I don't recall what was the thought process for the following, but he decides to hire the services of an erotic masseuse in her late thirties, whose body reminds him of his mother when he was a child. They have a nice enough time, she jerks him off, she leaves. The next girl that enters his hotel room he intends to murder. He calls some business that provides S&amp;M services.<br /><br />We are introduced to the other POV of this story, the S&amp;M prostitute that will face our murderous protagonist. She's lying in bed as her body dissolves the effects of the high dose of tranquilizers she had taken, that she uses in order to get some sleep. The author focuses on her pierced nipple, and how she tends to toy with it to regain control when the world becomes chaotic. She's one of those fucked up people who associate pain, particularly self induced pain, with control. She also has heavily scarred thighs to prove it. The author attempts to develop something of an obsessive compulsive disorder for her in the laziest way: making her count anything countable in the most obnoxiously precise manner possible. Curiously, though, the author immediately abandons this trait after the initial sequence. Who knows. In any case, the girl is called to service the protagonist, and she sets off towards the hotel.<br /><br />Heavier spoilers from now on. When the girl arrives at the hotel, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[a random somewhat intimidating guy looks in her direction and briefly approaches her to pick up something she had dropped. She suffers a panic attack. Having slipped into something of a psychosis, she locks herself in the bathroom and relives the sexual abuse she endured at the hands of her father. Plenty of descriptions of the old man licking her barely developed child clit. Afterwards he usually forced her to suck him off. When his father finally confessed to his wife, the woman convinced herself that her daughter was to blame, and from then on she was treated as filthy garbage that should apologize for being alive. She has developed a far worse dissociative disorder than the protagonist. The author elaborates on how she went depersonalized during the abuse and from then on at different periods of her life she felt like she watched the events while hanging from the roof of whatever room she happened to be in. I happen to be experienced in depersonalization myself; growing up in conflictive schools as a yet undiagnosed autistic person with inept parents, slipping in and out of psychosis to the extent that I had to cut out of my life anyone I knew from back then, my mind retreated into depersonalization whenever the anxiety got too bad, which was often. I recall me walking outside while watching my body in the third person stumbling through the crowds. I saw everything from the bottom of a well while I was somehow maneuvering that body, although I couldn't feel anything. Since I became 20 or 21 years old I haven't felt that again, so I have that going for me I guess <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />The girl <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[finally snaps out of her panic attack enough to remember that she has a job to do. When she meets the protagonist, she's weirded out by the contrast between the guy's poised demeanour and his cheap attire. She takes him for a germaphobe as he maneuvers to leave as few fingerprints as possible. The author proceeds through the rest of the novel by alternating between these two POVs in every scene. I would consider that a mistake in general, but for this story we need to understand how they process these events from their perspective, as most of what unfolds involves each person confusing the other's intentions (a big no-no in plotting: don't make your plot rely on some confusion that the main characters can resolve by just having a conversation) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />The girl attempts to get the ball rolling by <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[masturbating in front of him, but he finds it so lame that he laughs. She's hurt and quickly retreats into another panic attack. This one ends up slipping into full blown psychosis. The protagonist waits for fifty minutes in the room as the girl pretends to take a shower. When he finally enters the bathroom to find out what the fuck she's wasting his money for, he finds her naked and stabbing her thigh with some knife she carried in her purse. She's bleeding bad, as she's taken chunks of flesh out. The protagonist worries but not for her well-being: he's hiding murder implements as well as a confession in his bags, and here is a girl that could yell and attract the police at any point. She might even attempt to blame him for her wounds. He changes his plans: he can no longer murder her. He needs to get rid of his weapons, carry this girl to a hospital and leave town. As he walks down the hotel floor towards some garbage bin, the girl, naked and bleeding, wanders out of the room. The protagonist freaks out, forces her inside and struggles to make her understand that he'll take her to a hospital. Around that time she bit hard into his hand, tearing flesh out of it. In her psychosis, she "realizes" that this man must be the mysterious saviour that had ended up taking her to the hospital in the previous times she had hurt herself bad (later on she admits to herself that nobody had taken her to the hospital before, that she just regained consciousness and cursed her luck for remaining alive. She just wished that someone would care enough for her to carry her to a hospital). When she dresses herself and they both leave the room, he hails a cab. She snuggles up to him, hoping that he'll accompany her to her apartment later and never leave her side. After a doctor treats her, she believed that the protagonist would have bailed, but he's outside. During the scuffle in the hotel he had found his bag open, and the notebook where he had elaborated on his murderous intentions was open on the floor. He intends to figure out if this girl has read it. She hadn't, though: she had just flung the bag towards the wall in the middle of her psychosis <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[In any case she invites him to her apartment. She intends to make him into her boyfriend/caretaker. As she babbles on about food and furniture, she prepares him something to eat. She's afraid that he'll leave in a while, so, as she had done in the past with other "boyfriends", she crushes some horse tranquilizers into his food, which will make him wake up in a good bunch of hours next to her in bed. As the drugs are taking effect, she asks him who he really is, and what his intentions to come to town had really been. He believes that she's admitting that she's read the notebook, but the girl believes that he's some rich guy in search of companionship that his normal life doesn't allow. As he resolves to kill her, the drugs drag him to the floor. Before he loses consciousness he calls out for his girlfriend/wife back home to help him. Upon hearing another woman's name, the S&amp;M girl loses it and bashes his face and other parts of his body with a can opener. They struggle with each other as he attempts to get his murderous implements. By that point we are immersed in the elaborate perspectives of two psychotic people. He is in a trance, believing that he's fighting back against his mother, and she's out of her mind as usual. He ends up straddling her and lifting his arms to plunge the ice pick into her tight navel, only for him to finally pass out <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />While the protagonist <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[sleeps under the influence of the drugs, she realizes that this guy isn't her savior, but just another freak pretty much like her, that will leave forever back to his regular life. To control the pain, she commemorates the occasion by piercing her other nipple. The protagonist wakes up around this time and asks her what she's doing. The girl states the obvious. We are left without a denouement again (sort of calling card of this author, it seems). I guess the lesson in all this madness is that such fucked up people will get no respite, and that they are doomed to tumble through life in a variety of dazes until they die <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />The most unbelievable part of this story for me was the fact that someone as fucked up as that S&amp;M girl, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[constantly slipping in and out of psychosis, would have been able to hold down any job, let alone one that involves meeting seedy strangers and reliving her past sexual abuse over and over. I was tempted to lower the rating for this reason, but I did find her a interesting character. However, she's the kind of crazy that isn't endearing nor relatable. One moment she'd be all over you proclaiming eternal love, and the next moment she'd be pushing the pointy end of a knife through your eyeball. Best to steer clear of such people if you value your eyeballs <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />I'm already reading another one of his novels, but I'll spend the next two weeks working from home, where I have plenty other distractions generally less demanding than reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6534071109061935853" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sUOi2NcmzdbM7i3EU7n6ueycwYLUt6Zkp49aVU2xRLWLZcsKbHgEvmvFmaWkTPBmOsMmSho6KAP88RzkgPK2Lw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('B5w69qWaWpzwhfoKHjo/3gN9yFT6wXIZLN8mFc/oR2c9ulMkHsST9FeuTmvpzzUB1SIvnDRM/H53oWCkAqu1/Q==')})
  }

hierarchy decided their fates — and follow them through the years as they struggle against rigid societal conventions.\n\nShedding light on the most hidden precincts of Japanese society today, <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10004042556046874529">Ryū <b>Murakami</b> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11015261._' rel='nofollow noopener'>村上 龍</a>) is a Japanese novelist and filmmaker. He is not related to Haruki Murakami or Takashi Murakami.<br/><br/>Murakami's first work, the short novel Almost Transparent Blue, written while he was still a student, deals with promiscuity and drug use among disaffected Japanese youth. Critically acclaimed as a new style of literature, it won the newcomer's literature prize in 1976 despite some ob</span>
  <span id="freeText10004042556046874529" style="display:none">Ryū <b>Murakami</b> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11015261._" rel="nofollow noopener">村上 龍</a>) is a Japanese novelist and filmmaker. He is not related to Haruki Murakami or Takashi Murakami.<br /><br />Murakami's first work, the short novel Almost Transparent Blue, written while he was still a student, deals with promiscuity and drug use among disaffected Japanese youth. Critically acclaimed as a new style of literature, it won the newcomer's literature prize in 1976 despite some observers decrying it as decadent. Later the same year, Blue won the Akutagawa Prize, going on to become a best seller. In 1980, Murakami published the much longer novel Coin Locker Babies, again to critical acclaim.<br /><br />Takashi Miike's feature film Audition (1999) was based on one of his novels. Murakami reportedly liked it so much he gave Miike his blessing to adapt Coin Locker Babies. The screen play was worked on by director Jordan Galland. However, Miike could not raise funding for the project. An adaptation directed by Michele Civetta is currently in production.<br /><br />Murakami has played drums for a rock group called Coelacanth and hosted a TV talk show.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10004042556046874529" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Who knew there were still people like that in this world, though? Everybody wants to talk about themselves, and everybody wants to hear everybody else's story, so we take turns playing reporter and celebrity. 'It must have made you very sad when your own father raped you - can you describe some of your feelings at the time? Yes, I wept and wept, wonder why something like this had to happen to me'. It's like that. Everyone's running around comparing wounds, like bodybuilders showing off their muscles. And what's really unbelievable is that they really believe they can heal the wounds like that, just by putting them on display.&rdquo;</span>

If it were truly only for chopping ice, you'd think a completely different design might do. The people who produce and sell things like this don't understand, he thought. They don't realize that some of us break out in a cold sweat at just a glimpse of that shiny, pointed tip.&rdquo;</span>

        script.src = load[i];
        first.parentNode.insertBefore(script, first);
      }
    }
  if (w.attachEvent) {
    w.attachEvent('onload', go);
  } else {
    w.addEventListener('load', go, false);
  }
}(window, document,
    ['//assets.pinterest.com/js/pinit.js',
     '//apis.google.com/js/plusone.js'
    ]
  ));

//]]>
</script>
